expectancy, higher infant mortality and death rates, lower population and growth rates, and changes in the distribution of population by age and sex than would otherwise be expected.</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Mozambique</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/o\xca\x8a/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">o\xca\x8a</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/\xc3\xa6/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">\xc3\xa6</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/i\xcb\x90/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">i\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span></span>/</a></span></span>), officially the <b>Republic of Mozambique</b> (<a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>: <i lang="pt">Mo\xc3\xa7ambique</i> or <i><i lang="pt" title="Portuguese language text">Rep\xc3\xbablica de Mo\xc3\xa7ambique</i></i>, <small>pronounced&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Portuguese" title="Help:IPA/Portuguese">[\xca\x81\xc9\x9b\xcb\x88pu\xce\xb2lik\xc9\x90 \xc3\xb0\xc9\xa8 mus\xc9\x90\xcc\x83\xcb\x88bik\xc9\xa8]</a></span>) is a country in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Africa" title="Southeast Africa">Southeast Africa</a> bordered by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> to the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> to the west, and <a href="/wiki/Swaziland" title="Swaziland">Swaziland (Eswatini)</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> to the southwest. It is separated from the <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayotte" title="Mayotte">Mayotte</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Mozambique_Channel" title="Mozambique Channel">Mozambique Channel</a> to the east. The capital of Mozambique is <a href="/wiki/Maputo" title="Maputo">Maputo</a> (formerly known as "Louren\xc3\xa7o Marques" from 1876 to 1976) while <a href="/wiki/Matola" title="Matola">Matola</a> is the largest city, being a suburb of Maputo. \n</p><p>Between the first and fifth centuries AD, <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a>-speaking peoples migrated to present-day Mozambique from farther north and west. Beginning in the 11th century, Arab, Persian, and Somali merchants began settlements and establishing commercial ports along the coast, contributing to the development of a distinct <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">language</a>.\n</p><p>The voyage of <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> in 1498 marked the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a>, who began a gradual process of colonisation and settlement in 1505. After over four centuries of <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Portuguese rule</a>, Mozambique <a href="/wiki/Mozambican_War_of_Independence" title="Mozambican War of Independence">gained independence</a> in 1975, becoming the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Mozambique" title="People&#39;s Republic of Mozambique">People\'s Republic of Mozambique</a> shortly thereafter. After only two years of independence, the country descended into an intense and protracted <a href="/wiki/Mozambican_Civil_War" title="Mozambican Civil War">civil war</a> lasting from 1977 to 1992. In 1994, Mozambique held its first multiparty elections, and has since remained a relatively stable presidential republic, although it still faces a <a href="/wiki/RENAMO_insurgency_(2013%E2%80%93present)" title="RENAMO insurgency (2013\xe2\x80\x93present)">low-intensity insurgency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Mozambique is endowed with rich and extensive natural resources. The country\'s economy is based largely on agriculture, but industry is growing, mainly food and beverages, chemical manufacturing and aluminium and petroleum production. The tourism sector is also expanding. South Africa is Mozambique\'s main trading partner and source of <a href="/wiki/Foreign_direct_investment" title="Foreign direct investment">foreign direct investment</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> are also among the country\'s most important economic partners. Since 2001, Mozambique\'s annual average GDP growth has been among the world\'s highest. However, the country is still one of the poorest and most <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">underdeveloped countries</a> in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> ranking low in <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP per capita</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">human development</a>, measures of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_inequality-adjusted_HDI" title="List of countries by inequality-adjusted HDI">inequality</a> and average <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The only official language of Mozambique is <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, which is spoken mostly as a second language by about half the population. Common native languages include <a href="/wiki/Makhuwa_language" title="Makhuwa language">Makhuwa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sena_language" title="Sena language">Sena</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>. The country\'s population of around 29 million is composed overwhelmingly of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu people</a>. The largest religion in Mozambique is Christianity, with significant minorities following Islam and African traditional religions. Mozambique is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_the_Islamic_Cooperation" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation of the Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of the Islamic Cooperation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Portuguese_Language_Countries" title="Community of Portuguese Language Countries">Community of Portuguese Language Countries</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_African_Development_Community" title="Southern African Development Community">Southern African Development Community</a>, and is an observer at <a href="/wiki/Organisation_internationale_de_la_Francophonie" title="Organisation internationale de la Francophonie">La Francophonie</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The country was named Mo\xc3\xa7ambique by the Portuguese after the <a href="/wiki/Island_of_Mozambique" title="Island of Mozambique">Island of Mozambique</a>, derived from <i><a href="/wiki/Mussa_Bin_Bique" title="Mussa Bin Bique">Mussa Bin Bique</a></i> or <i>Musa Al Big</i> or <i>Mossa Al Bique</i> or <i>Mussa Ben Mbiki</i> or <i>Mussa Ibn Malik</i>, an <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a> trader who first visited the island and later lived there.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The island-town was the capital of the Portuguese colony until 1898, when it was moved south to Louren\xc3\xa7o Marques (now <a href="/wiki/Maputo" title="Maputo">Maputo</a>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mozambique" title="History of Mozambique">History of Mozambique</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mozambique_(3912235854).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Mozambique_%283912235854%29.jpg/220px-Mozambique_%283912235854%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Mozambique_%283912235854%29.jpg/330px-Mozambique_%283912235854%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Mozambique_%283912235854%29.jpg/440px-Mozambique_%283912235854%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1861" data-file-height="1246" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mozambique_(3912235854).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mozambican <a href="/wiki/Dhow" title="Dhow">dhow</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bantu_migrations">Bantu migrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Bantu migrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a></div>\n<p>Between the 1st and 5th centuries AD, waves of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a>-speaking people migrated from the west and north through the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Zambezi River">Zambezi River</a> valley and then gradually into the plateau and coastal areas.<sup id="cite_ref-BilateralRelationsFactSheet_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BilateralRelationsFactSheet-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> They established agricultural communities or societies based on herding cattle. They brought with them the technology for smelting and smithing iron.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Swahili_Coast">Swahili Coast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Swahili Coast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/220px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/330px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/440px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="602" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab-Swahili slave traders</a> and their captives on the <a href="/wiki/Ruvuma_River" title="Ruvuma River">Ruvuma River</a></div></div></div>\n<p>From the late first millennium AD, vast <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade" title="Indian Ocean trade">Indian Ocean trade</a> networks extended as far south into Mozambique as the ancient port town of <a href="/wiki/Chibuene" title="Chibuene">Chibuene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sinclair2_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair2-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in the 9th century, a growing involvement in Indian Ocean trade led to the development of numerous port towns along the entire East African coast, including modern day Mozambique. Largely autonomous, these towns broadly participated in the incipient <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili culture</a>. Islam was often adopted by urban elites, facilitating trade. In Mozambique, <a href="/wiki/Sofala" title="Sofala">Sofala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angoche" title="Angoche">Angoche</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Mozambique Island">Mozambique Island</a> were regional powers by the 15th century. \n</p><p>The towns traded with merchants from both the African interior and the broader Indian Ocean world. Particularly important were the gold and ivory caravan routes. Inland states like the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Zimbabwe" title="Kingdom of Zimbabwe">Kingdom of Zimbabwe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mutapa" title="Kingdom of Mutapa">Kingdom of Mutapa</a> provided the coveted gold and ivory, which were then exchanged up the coast to larger port cities like <a href="/wiki/Kilwa" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilwa">Kilwa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Portuguese_Mozambique_.281498.E2.80.931975.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_Mozambique_(1498\xe2\x80\x931975)">Portuguese Mozambique (1498\xe2\x80\x931975)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Portuguese Mozambique (1498\xe2\x80\x931975)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Portuguese Mozambique</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mozambique_n2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mozambique_n2.jpg/250px-Mozambique_n2.jpg" width="250" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mozambique_n2.jpg/375px-Mozambique_n2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mozambique_n2.jpg/500px-Mozambique_n2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1472" data-file-height="962" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mozambique_n2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Island_of_Mozambique" title="Island of Mozambique">Island of Mozambique</a> is a small coral island at the mouth of Mossuril Bay on the <a href="/wiki/Nacala" title="Nacala">Nacala</a> coast of northern Mozambique, first explored by Europeans in the late 15th century.</div></div></div>\n<p>From about 1500, Portuguese trading posts and forts displaced the Arabic commercial and military hegemony, becoming regular ports of call on the new European sea route to the east.<sup id="cite_ref-BilateralRelationsFactSheet_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BilateralRelationsFactSheet-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The voyage of <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> in 1498 marked the Portuguese entry into trade, politics, and society of the region. The Portuguese gained control of the <a href="/wiki/Island_of_Mozambique" title="Island of Mozambique">Island of Mozambique</a> and the port city of <a href="/wiki/Sofala" title="Sofala">Sofala</a> in the early 16th century, and by the 1530s, small groups of Portuguese traders and <a href="/wiki/Prospecting" title="Prospecting">prospectors</a> seeking gold penetrated the interior regions, where they set up garrisons and trading posts at <a href="/wiki/Sena,_Mozambique" class="mw-redirect" title="Sena, Mozambique">Sena</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tete" class="mw-redirect" title="Tete">Tete</a> on the River <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> and tried to gain exclusive control over the gold trade.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the central part of the Mozambique territory, the Portuguese attempted to legitimise and consolidate their trade and settlement positions through the creation of <i>prazos</i> (land grants) tied to their settlement and administration. While <i>prazos</i> were originally developed to be held by Portuguese, through intermarriage they became African Portuguese or African Indian centres defended by large African slave armies known as <i><a href="/wiki/Kunda_people" title="Kunda people">Chikunda</a></i>. \nHistorically within Mozambique there was slavery. Human beings were bought and sold by African tribal chiefs, first to Arab Muslim traders and sent to Middle East Asia cities and plantations, and later to Portuguese and other European traders as well. Many Mozambican slaves were supplied by tribal chiefs who raided warring tribes and sold their captives to the <i>prazeiros</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lourenco-Marques-pc-c1905.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Lourenco-Marques-pc-c1905.jpg/220px-Lourenco-Marques-pc-c1905.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Lourenco-Marques-pc-c1905.jpg/330px-Lourenco-Marques-pc-c1905.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Lourenco-Marques-pc-c1905.jpg/440px-Lourenco-Marques-pc-c1905.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="380" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lourenco-Marques-pc-c1905.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of the Central Avenue in Louren\xc3\xa7o Marques, now Maputo, ca. 1905;</div></div></div>\n<p>Although Portuguese influence gradually expanded, its power was limited and exercised through individual settlers and officials who were granted extensive autonomy. The Portuguese were able to wrest much of the coastal trade from Arab Muslims between 1500 and 1700, but, with the Arab Muslim seizure of Portugal\'s key foothold at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Jesus" title="Fort Jesus">Fort Jesus</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mombasa_Island" title="Mombasa Island">Mombasa Island</a> (now in Kenya) in 1698, the pendulum began to swing in the other direction. As a result, investment lagged while <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> devoted itself to the more lucrative trade with India and the Far East and to the colonisation of Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-BilateralRelationsFactSheet_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BilateralRelationsFactSheet-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During these wars, the <a href="/wiki/Mazrui" title="Mazrui">Mazrui</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Oman" title="History of Oman">Omani Arabs</a> reclaimed much of the Indian Ocean trade, forcing the Portuguese to retreat south. Many <i>prazos</i> had declined by the mid-19th century, but several of them survived. During the 19th century other European powers, particularly the British (<a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Company" title="British South Africa Company">British South Africa Company</a>) and the French (Madagascar), became increasingly involved in the trade and politics of the region around the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_East_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Portuguese East Africa">Portuguese East African</a> territories.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TT_CMZ-AF-GT_E_2-1_10_81_-_Oficina_de_Tipografia_da_Escola_de_Artes_e_Of%C3%ADcios.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/TT_CMZ-AF-GT_E_2-1_10_81_-_Oficina_de_Tipografia_da_Escola_de_Artes_e_Of%C3%ADcios.jpg/250px-TT_CMZ-AF-GT_E_2-1_10_81_-_Oficina_de_Tipografia_da_Escola_de_Artes_e_Of%C3%ADcios.jpg" width="250" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/TT_CMZ-AF-GT_E_2-1_10_81_-_Oficina_de_Tipografia_da_Escola_de_Artes_e_Of%C3%ADcios.jpg/375px-TT_CMZ-AF-GT_E_2-1_10_81_-_Oficina_de_Tipografia_da_Escola_de_Artes_e_Of%C3%ADcios.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/TT_CMZ-AF-GT_E_2-1_10_81_-_Oficina_de_Tipografia_da_Escola_de_Artes_e_Of%C3%ADcios.jpg/500px-TT_CMZ-AF-GT_E_2-1_10_81_-_Oficina_de_Tipografia_da_Escola_de_Artes_e_Of%C3%ADcios.jpg 2x" data-file-width="910" data-file-height="700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TT_CMZ-AF-GT_E_2-1_10_81_-_Oficina_de_Tipografia_da_Escola_de_Artes_e_Of%C3%ADcios.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portuguese language printing and typesetting class, 1930</div></div></div>\n<p>By the early 20th century the Portuguese had shifted the administration of much of Mozambique to large private companies, like the <a href="/wiki/Mozambique_Company" title="Mozambique Company">Mozambique Company</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Zambezia_Province" title="Zambezia Province">Zambezia Company</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Niassa_Company" title="Niassa Company">Niassa Company</a>, controlled and financed mostly by "British" financiers such as <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Joel" title="Solomon Joel">Solomon Joel</a>, which established railroad lines to their neighbouring colonies (South Africa and Rhodesia). Although slavery had been legally abolished in Mozambique, at the end of the 19th century the Chartered companies enacted a forced labour policy and supplied cheap\xe2\x80\x94often forced\xe2\x80\x94African labour to the <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantations</a> of the nearby British colonies and South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-BilateralRelationsFactSheet_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BilateralRelationsFactSheet-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The Zambezia Company, the most profitable chartered company, took over a number of smaller <i>prazeiro</i> holdings, and established military outposts to protect its property. The chartered companies built roads and ports to bring their goods to market including a railroad linking present day Zimbabwe with the Mozambican port of <a href="/wiki/Beira,_Mozambique" title="Beira, Mozambique">Beira</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Cambridge_history_of_Africa_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cambridge_history_of_Africa-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Third_Portuguese_Empire,_1825-1975_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Third_Portuguese_Empire,_1825-1975-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to their unsatisfactory performance and the shift, under the <a href="/wiki/Corporatist" class="mw-redirect" title="Corporatist">corporatist</a> <a href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Portugal)" title="Estado Novo (Portugal)">Estado Novo</a> regime of <a href="/wiki/Oliveira_Salazar" class="mw-redirect" title="Oliveira Salazar">Oliveira Salazar</a>, towards a stronger Portuguese control of <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>\'s economy, the companies\' concessions were not renewed when they ran out. This was what happened in 1942 with the Mozambique Company, which however continued to operate in the agricultural and commercial sectors as a corporation, and had already happened in 1929 with the termination of the Niassa Company\'s concession. In 1951, the Portuguese overseas colonies in Africa were rebranded as Overseas Provinces of Portugal.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Cambridge_history_of_Africa_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cambridge_history_of_Africa-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Third_Portuguese_Empire,_1825-1975_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Third_Portuguese_Empire,_1825-1975-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Mozambican_War_of_Independence_.281964.E2.80.931974.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mozambican_War_of_Independence_(1964\xe2\x80\x931974)">Mozambican War of Independence (1964\xe2\x80\x931974)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mozambican War of Independence (1964\xe2\x80\x931974)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sempreatentos...aoperigo!.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg/250px-Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg" width="250" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg/375px-Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg/500px-Sempreatentos...aoperigo%21.jpg 2x" data-file-width="511" data-file-height="437" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sempreatentos...aoperigo!.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portuguese troops during the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Colonial_War" title="Portuguese Colonial War">Portuguese Colonial War</a>, some loading <a href="/wiki/FN_FAL" title="FN FAL">FN FAL</a> and G3.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mozambican_War_of_Independence" title="Mozambican War of Independence">Mozambican War of Independence</a></div>\n<p>As <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-colonial" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-colonial">anti-colonial</a> ideologies spread out across Africa, many clandestine political movements were established in support of Mozambican independence. These movements claimed that since policies and development plans were primarily designed by the ruling authorities for the benefit of Mozambique\'s Portuguese population, little attention was paid to Mozambique\'s tribal integration and the development of its native communities.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the official guerrilla statements, this affected a majority of the indigenous population who suffered both state-sponsored discrimination and enormous social pressure. Many felt they had received too little opportunity or resources to upgrade their skills and improve their economic and social situation to a degree comparable to that of the Europeans. Statistically, Mozambique\'s Portuguese whites were indeed wealthier and more skilled than the black indigenous majority. As a response to the guerrilla movement, the Portuguese government from the 1960s and principally the early 1970s, initiated gradual changes with new socioeconomic developments and egalitarian policies for all.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The Front for the Liberation of Mozambique (<a href="/wiki/FRELIMO" title="FRELIMO">FRELIMO</a>) initiated a guerrilla campaign against Portuguese rule in September 1964. This conflict\xe2\x80\x94along with the two others already initiated in the other Portuguese colonies of <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Angola" title="Portuguese Angola">Angola</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Guinea" title="Portuguese Guinea">Portuguese Guinea</a>\xe2\x80\x94became part of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Colonial_War" title="Portuguese Colonial War">Portuguese Colonial War</a> (1961\xe2\x80\x931974). From a military standpoint, the Portuguese regular army maintained control of the population centres while the guerrilla forces sought to undermine their influence in rural and tribal areas in the north and west. As part of their response to FRELIMO, the Portuguese government began to pay more attention to creating favourable conditions for social development and economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Independence_.281975.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_(1975)">Independence (1975)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Independence (1975)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>FRELIMO took control of the territory after 10 years of sporadic warfare, as well as Portugal\'s own return to democracy after the fall of the authoritarian <a href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Portugal)" title="Estado Novo (Portugal)">Estado Novo</a> regime the <a href="/wiki/Carnation_Revolution" title="Carnation Revolution">Carnation Revolution</a> of April 1974, and the failed <a href="/wiki/Coup_of_25_November_1975" title="Coup of 25 November 1975">coup of 25 November 1975</a>. Within a year, most of the 250,000 Portuguese in Mozambique had left\xe2\x80\x94some expelled by the government of the nearly independent territory, some fleeing in fear\xe2\x80\x94and Mozambique became independent from Portugal on 25 June 1975. A law had been passed on the initiative of the relatively unknown <a href="/wiki/Armando_Guebuza" title="Armando Guebuza">Armando Guebuza</a> of the FRELIMO party, ordering the Portuguese to leave the country in 24 hours with only 20 kilograms (44 <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pounds</a>) of luggage. Unable to salvage any of their assets, most of them returned to Portugal penniless.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Mozambican_Civil_War_.281977.E2.80.931992.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mozambican_Civil_War_(1977\xe2\x80\x931992)">Mozambican Civil War (1977\xe2\x80\x931992)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mozambican Civil War (1977\xe2\x80\x931992)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mozambican_Civil_War" title="Mozambican Civil War">Mozambican Civil War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Land_mine_victim_2_(4364914733).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Land_mine_victim_2_%284364914733%29.jpg/220px-Land_mine_victim_2_%284364914733%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Land_mine_victim_2_%284364914733%29.jpg/330px-Land_mine_victim_2_%284364914733%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Land_mine_victim_2_%284364914733%29.jpg/440px-Land_mine_victim_2_%284364914733%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Land_mine_victim_2_(4364914733).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A land mine victim in Mozambique</div></div></div>\n<p>The new government under president <a href="/wiki/Samora_Machel" title="Samora Machel">Samora Machel</a> established a <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party state</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> principles. It received diplomatic and some military support from <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and proceeded to crack down on opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Starting shortly after the independence, the country was plagued from 1977 to 1992 by a long and violent civil war between the opposition forces of anti-Communist Mozambican National Resistance (<a href="/wiki/RENAMO" title="RENAMO">RENAMO</a>) rebel militias and the FRELIMO regime. This conflict characterised the first decades of Mozambican independence, combined with sabotage from the neighbouring states of <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a> and South Africa, ineffective policies, failed central planning, and the resulting economic collapse. This period was also marked by the exodus of Portuguese nationals and Mozambicans of Portuguese heritage,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> a collapsed infrastructure, lack of investment in productive assets, and government nationalisation of privately owned industries, as well as widespread famine.\n</p><p>During most of the civil war, the FRELIMO-formed central government was unable to exercise effective control outside of urban areas, many of which were cut off from the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-BilateralRelationsFactSheet_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BilateralRelationsFactSheet-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> RENAMO-controlled areas included up to 50% of the rural areas in several provinces, and it is reported that health services of any kind were isolated from assistance for years in those areas. The problem worsened when the government cut back spending on health care.<sup id="cite_ref-Pfeiffer._J._2003_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pfeiffer._J._2003-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The war was marked by mass human rights violations from both sides of the conflict, with RENAMO contributing to the chaos through the use of terror and indiscriminate targeting of civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The central government executed tens of thousands of people while trying to extend its control throughout the country and sent many people to "re-education camps" where thousands died.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RhodesiaAllies1975.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/RhodesiaAllies1975.png/220px-RhodesiaAllies1975.png" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/RhodesiaAllies1975.png/330px-RhodesiaAllies1975.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/RhodesiaAllies1975.png/440px-RhodesiaAllies1975.png 2x" data-file-width="624" data-file-height="475" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RhodesiaAllies1975.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The geopolitical situation in 1975, nations friendly to the FRELIMO are shown in orange</div></div></div>\n<p>During the war, RENAMO proposed a peace agreement based on the secession of RENAMO-controlled northern and western territories as the independent <i>Republic of Rombesia</i>, but FRELIMO refused, insisting on the undivided sovereignty of the entire country. An estimated one million Mozambicans perished during the civil war, 1.7 million took refuge in neighbouring states, and several million more were internally displaced.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The FRELIMO regime also gave shelter and support to South African (<a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a>) and Zimbabwean (<a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe_African_National_Union" title="Zimbabwe African National Union">Zimbabwe African National Union</a>) rebel movements, while the governments of Rhodesia and later South Africa (at that time still apartheid) backed RENAMO in the civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-BilateralRelationsFactSheet_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BilateralRelationsFactSheet-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 19 October 1986, Samora Machel was on his way back from an international meeting in <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> in the presidential <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-134" title="Tupolev Tu-134">Tupolev Tu-134</a> aircraft when the <a href="/wiki/1986_Mozambican_Tupolev_Tu-134_crash" title="1986 Mozambican Tupolev Tu-134 crash">plane crashed</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Lebombo_Mountains" title="Lebombo Mountains">Lebombo Mountains</a> near <a href="/wiki/Mbuzini" title="Mbuzini">Mbuzini</a>. There were ten survivors, but President Machel and thirty-three others died, including ministers and officials of the Mozambique government. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>\' <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> delegation issued a minority report contending that their expertise and experience had been undermined by the South Africans. Representatives of the Soviet Union advanced the theory that the plane had been intentionally diverted by a false <a href="/wiki/VHF_omnidirectional_range" title="VHF omnidirectional range">navigational beacon</a> signal, using a technology provided by military intelligence operatives of the South African government.<sup id="cite_ref-TRC_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TRC-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Machel\'s successor <a href="/wiki/Joaquim_Chissano" title="Joaquim Chissano">Joaquim Chissano</a> implemented sweeping changes in the country, starting reforms such as changing from Marxism to capitalism, and began peace talks with RENAMO. The new constitution enacted in 1990 provided for a <a href="/wiki/Multi-party_system" title="Multi-party system">multi-party political system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market-based economy</a>, and free elections. The civil war ended in October 1992 with the <a href="/wiki/Rome_General_Peace_Accords" title="Rome General Peace Accords">Rome General Peace Accords</a>, first brokered by the Christian Council of Mozambique (Council of Protestant Churches) and then taken over by <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Sant%27Egidio" title="Community of Sant&#39;Egidio">Community of Sant\'Egidio</a>. Peace returned to Mozambique, under supervision of the <a href="/wiki/ONUMOZ" class="mw-redirect" title="ONUMOZ">ONUMOZ</a> peacekeeping force of the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BilateralRelationsFactSheet_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BilateralRelationsFactSheet-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Democratic_era_.281993.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic_era_(1993\xe2\x80\x93present)">Democratic era (1993\xe2\x80\x93present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Democratic era (1993\xe2\x80\x93present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helicopter_over_flooded_Central_Mozambique.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Helicopter_over_flooded_Central_Mozambique.jpg/220px-Helicopter_over_flooded_Central_Mozambique.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Helicopter_over_flooded_Central_Mozambique.jpg/330px-Helicopter_over_flooded_Central_Mozambique.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Helicopter_over_flooded_Central_Mozambique.jpg/440px-Helicopter_over_flooded_Central_Mozambique.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helicopter_over_flooded_Central_Mozambique.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A US helicopter flying over the flooded Limpopo River during the <a href="/wiki/2000_Mozambique_flood" title="2000 Mozambique flood">2000 Mozambique flood</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Mozambique held elections in 1994, which were accepted by most political parties as free and fair although still contested by many nationals and observers alike. <a href="/wiki/FRELIMO" title="FRELIMO">FRELIMO</a> won, under Joaquim Chissano, while <a href="/wiki/RENAMO" title="RENAMO">RENAMO</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Afonso_Dhlakama" title="Afonso Dhlakama">Afonso Dhlakama</a>, ran as the official opposition.\n</p><p>In 1995, Mozambique joined the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, becoming, at the time, the only member nation that had never been part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.\n</p><p>By mid-1995, over 1.7 million refugees who had sought asylum in neighbouring countries had returned to Mozambique, part of the largest repatriation witnessed in sub-Saharan Africa. An additional four million <a href="/wiki/Internally_displaced_person" title="Internally displaced person">internally displaced persons</a> had returned to their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-BilateralRelationsFactSheet_13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BilateralRelationsFactSheet-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 1999, Mozambique held elections for a second time since the civil war, which were again won by FRELIMO. RENAMO accused FRELIMO of fraud, and threatened to return to civil war, but backed down after taking the matter to the Supreme Court and losing.\n</p><p>In early 2000, a <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclone</a> caused <a href="/wiki/2000_Mozambique_flood" title="2000 Mozambique flood">widespread flooding</a> in the country, killing hundreds and devastating the already precarious infrastructure. There were widespread suspicions that foreign aid resources had been diverted by powerful leaders of FRELIMO. <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Cardoso_(journalist)" title="Carlos Cardoso (journalist)">Carlos Cardoso</a>, a journalist investigating these allegations, was murdered, and his death was never satisfactorily explained.\n</p><p>Indicating in 2001 that he would not run for a third term, Chissano criticised leaders who stayed on longer than he had, which was generally seen as a reference to <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambian</a> president <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Chiluba" title="Frederick Chiluba">Frederick Chiluba</a>, who at the time was considering a third term, and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwean</a> president <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a>, then in his fourth term. Presidential and National Assembly elections took place on 1\xe2\x80\x932 December 2004. FRELIMO candidate <a href="/wiki/Armando_Guebuza" title="Armando Guebuza">Armando Guebuza</a> won with 64% of the popular vote, while his opponent, <a href="/wiki/Afonso_Dhlakama" title="Afonso Dhlakama">Afonso Dhlakama</a> of RENAMO, received 32% of the popular vote. FRELIMO won 160 seats in Parliament, with a coalition of RENAMO and several small parties winning the 90 remaining seats. Guebuza was inaugurated as the President of Mozambique on 2 February 2005, and served two five-year terms. His successor, <a href="/wiki/Filipe_Nyusi" title="Filipe Nyusi">Filipe Nyusi</a>, became the 4th President of Mozambique on 15 January 2015.\n</p><p>Since 2013, a low-intensity <a href="/wiki/RENAMO_insurgency_(2013%E2%80%93present)" title="RENAMO insurgency (2013\xe2\x80\x93present)">insurgency by RENAMO</a> has been occurring, mainly in the country\'s central and northern regions. On 5 September 2014, former president Guebuza and the leader of RENAMO Dhlakama signed the Accord on Cessation of Hostilities, which brought the military hostilities to a halt and allowed both parties to concentrate on the general elections to be held in October 2014. However, after the general elections, a new political crisis emerged and the country appears to be once again on the brink of violent conflict. RENAMO does not recognise the validity of the election results, and demands the control of six provinces \xe2\x80\x93 Nampula, Niassa, Tete, Zambezia, Sofala, and Manica \xe2\x80\x93 where they claim to have won a majority.<sup id="cite_ref-:02_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> About 12,000 refugees are now in neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/UNHCR" class="mw-redirect" title="UNHCR">UNHCR</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doctors_Without_Borders" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctors Without Borders">Doctors Without Borders</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a> have reported that government forces have torched villages and carried out <a href="/wiki/Summary_execution" title="Summary execution">summary executions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wartime_sexual_violence" title="Wartime sexual violence">sexual abuses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_climate">Geography and climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mozambique&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geography and climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Mozambique" title="Geography of Mozambique">Geography of Mozambique</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mozambique_sat.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Mozambique_sat.png/100px-Mozambique_sat.png" width="100" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Mozambique_sat.png/150px-Mozambique_sat.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Mozambique_sat.png/200px-Mozambique_sat.png 2x" data-file-width="1277" data-file-height="1968" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mozambique_sat.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite image</div></div></div>\n<p>At 309,475&#160;sq&#160;mi (801,537&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), Mozambique is the world\'s 36th-largest country. It is comparable in size to Turkey. Mozambique is located on the southeast coast of Africa. It is bound by Swaziland to the south, South Africa to the southwest, Zimbabwe to the west, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> to the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> to the north and the Indian Ocean to the east. Mozambique lies between latitudes <a href="/wiki/10th_parallel_south" title="10th parallel south">10\xc2\xb0</a> and <a href="/wiki/27th_parallel_south" title="27th parallel south">27\xc2\xb0S</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/30th_meridian_east" title="30th meridian east">30\xc2\xb0</a> and <a href="/wiki/41st_meridian_east" title="41st meridian east">41\xc2\xb0E</a>.\n</p><p>The country is divided into two topographical regions by the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Zambezi River">Zambezi River</a>. To the north of the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> River, the narrow coastal strip gives way to inland hills and low plateaus. Rugged highlands are further west; they include the <a href="/wiki/Niassa" class="mw-redirect" title="Niassa">Niassa</a> highlands, <a href="/wiki/Namuli" class="mw-redirect" title="Namuli">Namuli</a> or Shire highlands, Angonia highlands, <a href="/wiki/Tete" class="mw-redirect" title="Tete">Tete</a> highlands and the <a href="/wiki/Makonde_(District)" class="mw-redirect" title="Makonde (District)">Makonde</a> plateau, covered with <a href="/wiki/Miombo" title="Miombo">miombo</a> woodlands. To the south of the Zambezi River, the lowlands are broader with the Mashonaland plateau and <a href="/wiki/Lebombo_Mountains" title="Lebombo Mountains">Lebombo Mountains</a> located in the deep south.\n</p><p>The country is drained by five principal rivers and several smaller ones with the largest and most important the Zambezi. The country has four notable lakes: <a href="/wiki/Lake_Niassa" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake Niassa">Lake Niassa</a> (or Malawi), <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chiuta" title="Lake Chiuta">Lake Chiuta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Cahora_Bassa" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake Cahora Bassa">Lake Cahora Bassa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Shirwa" title="Lake Shirwa">Lake Shirwa</a>, all in the north. The major cities are <a href="/wiki/Maputo" title="Maputo">Maputo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beira,_Mozambique" title="Beira, Mozambique">Beira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nampula" title="Nampula">Nampula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tete" class="mw-redirect" title="Tete">Tete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quelimane" title="Quelimane">Quelimane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chimoio" title="Chimoio">Chimoio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pemba,_Mozambique" title="Pemba, Mozambique">Pemba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inhambane" title="Inhambane">Inhambane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xai-Xai" title="Xai-Xai">Xai-Xai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lichinga" title="Lichinga">Lichinga</a>.\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n\t<li class="gallerycaption">Geography of Mozambique</li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Lebombo_Mountains" title="Lebombo Mountains">Lebombo Mountains</a>.\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 160px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 160px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mt_Binga_Mozambique.JPG" class="image"><img alt=""
